Substances Are Never Devoid of Accidents
Proof that substances always have accidents via dichotomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why substances must always be accompanied by accidents like association, separation, motion, or rest, and how the author proves this necessity using rational argument.

Core Idea
Substances cannot exist without accidents; they are inseparable from motion, rest, association, or separation.

In this passage, the author builds on the proven origination of accidents to show that substances themselves are never free from them. He examines two pairs of accidents—association and separation, motion and rest—and argues that there is no third possibility between the two states. This establishes that accidents are essential concomitants of substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from establishing accidents to defining the four terms and then the logical argument.
The passage ends with an objection that the author will answer later.
2
Identify the definitions of the four accidents
Locate the definitions of association (اجتماع), separation (افتراق), motion (حركة), and rest (سكون). Notice how each is defined in relation to substances and place.
Association and separation are about the spatial relation between two substances; motion and rest about a single substance in place.
3
Understand the dichotomy argument
The author argues that for two substances, there is no intermediate between having an interval (separation) and not having one (association). Similarly, a substance in place must either move (motion) or stay (rest). Therefore, substances cannot be empty of these accidents.
The objection at the end questions how a substance at the moment of origination can be said to have motion or rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا ثبت حدوث الأعراض وتقرر، فتأملنا بعد ذلك في حال الأعيان فوجدناها غير متعربة من الأعراض التي ثبت حدوثها بما لا شبهة فيه من الدلائل، ثم تأملنا فوجدنا تعريها عن الأعراض وخلوها عنها ممتنعا مستحيلا، وذلك لأنا رأينا الاجتماع والافتراق معنيين وراء المفترق والمجتمع، وكذا الحركة والسكون على ما بينا في فصل إثبات الأعراض ثم رأينا أن الاجتماع تماس الجوهرين حتى لا يكون بينهما مكان والافتراق تباين الجوهرين حتى يكون لثالث بينهما مكان، وقيل الاجتماع كون جوهر بجنب جوهر بحيث ليس بينهما حيز، والافتراق كون جوهر لا بجنب جوهر، والحركة هي الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان، والسكون هو القرار في مكان في زمانين فصاعدا ثم رأينا أن وجود جوهرين لا يخلو من أن يكون بينهما حيزا أو لا يكون، ومن أن يكون كل واحد منهما بجنب صاحبه أو يكون لا بجنب صاحبه، ولا يتصور بين هاتين الحالتين واسطة، فكان إذا خلو الجوهرين عن الاجتماع والافتراق محالا، وكذا عن الحركة والسكون لأن المتمكن في مكان، إما أن ينتقل عنه فيكون متحركا وإما أن يستقر فيه فيكون ساكنا، ولا واسطة بينهما، فإذا كان خلو الجوهرين عنهما جميعا محال فإن قلت: أليس أن الجوهر عندكم في أول أحوال وجوده يكون خاليا عن الحركة والسكون جميعا، إذ هو عندكم في حال حدوثه ليس بمتحرك ولا ساكن، فما أنكرتم
Then, when the origination of accidents is established and confirmed, we afterwards contemplated the state of substances and found them not free from accidents whose origination has been established by proofs free of any doubt. Then we contemplated and found their being stripped of accidents and empty of them to be impossible and inconceivable. That is because we saw that association (اجتماع) and separation (افتراق) are two meanings beyond the separated and the associated, and likewise motion (حركة) and rest (سكون) as we explained in the chapter on establishing accidents. Then we saw that association is the contact of two substances such that there is no space between them, and separation is the divergence of two substances such that there is a space for a third between them. And it was said: association is a substance being adjacent to a substance such that there is no interval between them, and separation is a substance not being adjacent to a substance. Motion is the transfer from one place to another, and rest is remaining in a place for two times or more. Then we saw that the existence of two substances is not free from either having an interval between them or not, and from each one being adjacent to its companion or not adjacent. And there is no conceivable intermediate between these two states. So if the two substances were empty of association and separation, it would be impossible. And likewise [empty] of motion and rest, because a thing occupying a place either moves away from it, thus being in motion, or stays in it, thus being at rest, and there is no intermediate between them. So if the substances being empty of both is impossible, then if you say: Is it not the case that the substance, according to you, at the first moment of its existence is empty of both motion and rest, since at the moment of its origination it is neither moving nor resting? So what prevents you...
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a proof that substances (جواهر) are never devoid of accidents (أعراض). The author begins by affirming that accidents are proven to be originated (حادثة). Then he examines substances and finds that they are never free from these accidents. Specifically, he argues that any two substances must either be in a state of association (اجتماع) – meaning they are contiguous with no space between them – or separation (افتراق) – meaning there is space between them. There is no third possibility. Therefore, it is impossible for two substances to exist without one of these two accidents. Similarly, any substance occupying a place must either move from it (motion) or stay in it (rest), with no intermediate. Hence, substances cannot be empty of motion and rest. The author then raises an objection: a substance at the moment of its origination is neither moving nor resting, so how can it be said to always have motion or rest? The passage cuts off here; the answer is given on the following pages. The proof establishes that accidents are essential concomitants of substances, and thus substances cannot exist without them.

Key Terms
الجواهر
الأعراض
الاجتماع
الافتراق
الحركة
السكون
المتمكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A substance at the moment of its creation has no accident, especially motion or rest.
Correction: The author argues that even at the first moment, a substance must have at least association or separation with respect to another substance, or else it would be inconceivable. The objection raises this point, but the answer (in later pages) shows that the substance does have the accident of 'being created' or similar.

Checkpoint
What two pairs of accidents does the author argue substances must always have?
A) Colour and shape
B) Association and separation, motion and rest
C) Life and knowledge
D) Heat and cold

Key Takeaway
Substances are never devoid of accidents; they always have either association or separation, and either motion or rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If substances cannot exist without accidents even for an instant, what does that imply about your own existence? Are you truly independent for even a moment? What does this constant dependency demand of you in your relationship with the Creator?
?
The proof shows that your body is never free from accidents like motion or rest. How does this awareness of constant change affect your sense of purpose? Where are you moving towards, and what are you resting in?
?
This passage cuts off with an objection about the first moment of existence. Consider your own first moment of existence: were you not dependent on Allah for that very instant? How does this truth change how you view your past, present, and future?

The Impossibility of a Substance Being Free from Motion or Rest in the State of Remaining
Al-Nasafi proves that a substance in the state of remaining necessarily has motion or rest, but the first state is exempt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's argument that the first moment of a substance's existence cannot be classified as motion or rest, while subsequent moments must be one or the other.

Core Idea
A substance in its first instant has a single kawn—neither motion nor rest.

Al-Nasafi presents a crucial argument for the temporality of substances. He distinguishes between the first moment of existence and all subsequent moments. In the state of remaining, a substance must be either moving or at rest, but the first state escapes that classification because it lacks a second kawn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the full literal translation. Let the structure of the argument become familiar before analyzing.
Notice how the author uses straightforward conditions: if in same place, rest; if different, motion.
2
Understand the definition of motion and rest
The author defines motion as two 'kawns' in two places, and rest as two 'kawns' in one place. Grasp this definition clearly.
Kawn (كون) is an accident of being in a place at a time.
3
Analyze the first state vs. state of remaining
Contrast the single kawn of the first state with the two kawns required for motion/rest. See why the first state cannot be classified as motion or rest.
The first state is a single instant without a prior referent for comparison.
4
See the argument's conclusion
Recognize that by admitting the first state as origination, one proves substances are temporal. The opponent cannot avoid this conclusion.
Even if they deny the first state qualifies as origination, they must hold that substances are never free from motion/rest, which is impossible in the first moment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه في الأزل يكون خاليا عنهما ؟ قيل عرف ببديهة العقل معرفة لا يعارضها شك أن الجوهر في حالة البقاء لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون لأنه إما أن يكون في الزمان الثاني في المكان الذي كان فيه الزمان الأول، وإما أن يكون في غيره فإن كان في المكان الأول فهذا سكون، وإن كان في المكان الثاني فهو حركة، ولا يتصور خلوه عن ذلك فأما في أول أحوال وجوده فتلك حالة واحدة لم يتصل بها حالة أخرى ليكون فيها في المكان الأول فيكون ساكنا أو في مكان آخر فيكون متحركا وفي حالة البقاء الحكم بخلافه، يوضحه أن الحركة هي كونان في مكانين والسكون كونان في مكان واحد، وفي الحالة الأولى لن يتصور إلا كون واحد وهو ليس بحركة ولا بسكون، فأما في حالة البقاء فيوجد كونان، فلابد من أن يكون حركة أو سكونا، فإن سلمتم للجوهر حالة الحدوث فقد أقررتم بحدوث الجواهر، فوقعت لنا الغنية عن إثباته بالدليل، وإن لم تسلموا ذلك فلن يتصور عندكم خلو الجواهر عن الحركة والسكون، على أن عندنا في أول أحوال الوجود لن يخلو الجوهر عن الكون وهو عرض أن كان خلا عن الحركة والسكون، لما أن كل واحد منهما عرضان وهما كونان ويستحيل وجود كونين في حالة واحدة ووجود كون واحد كاف لاستحالة خلوه عن العرض
That it in pre-eternity would be free from them? He said: Known by the primary intellect a knowledge not contested by doubt that the substance in the state of remaining is not free from motion and rest, because it is either in the second time in the place where it was in the first time, or it is in other than it. If it is in the first place, this is rest; if it is in the second place, it is motion. And it is not conceivable that it be free from that. As for the first of the states of its existence, that is a single state not connected to another state such that it would be in it in the first place so as to be at rest or in another place so as to be in motion. And in the state of remaining the judgment is contrary to that. He clarifies this by saying that motion is two 'kawns' in two places, and rest is two 'kawns' in one place. In the first state only one 'kawn' can be conceived, which is neither motion nor rest. As for the state of remaining, two 'kawns' exist, so it must be either motion or rest. If you admit for the substance a state of origination, then you have admitted the temporality of substances, and we have no need to prove it by evidence. And if you do not admit that, then according to you substances cannot be conceived free from motion and rest. Moreover, according to us in the first of the states of existence, the substance is not free from the 'kawn', which is an accident, even if it is free from motion and rest, because each of those two (motion and rest) are two accidents and are two 'kawns', and it is impossible for two 'kawns' to exist in a single state. The existence of a single 'kawn' suffices to prevent it from being free from an accident.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument showing that substances must be temporal because they never exist without accidents. The author begins by stating that a substance in the state of remaining (i.e., after it has come into existence and persists) cannot be free from either motion or rest. The reason is that in any given moment after the first, the substance must either remain in its previous location (which is rest) or change location (which is motion). There is no third possibility. However, the first moment of existence is different: it is a single instant without a prior moment, so it cannot be compared. Motion and rest are each defined as two 'kawns' (occurrences of being in a place). Motion is being in two places, rest is being in one place twice. In that first moment, only one 'kawn' exists, so it cannot be motion or rest. The author uses this to force the opponent to admit that the first state is a state of origination (hadith), which automatically proves the temporality of the substance. If the opponent denies that first state counts as origination, then they would have to hold that the substance is never free from motion/rest, which is impossible in the first moment. The author also notes that even if the first moment lacks motion/rest, it still contains a 'kawn' (the accident of being in a place), so the substance is always conjoined with an accident, which makes it temporal. This argument relies on the distinction between the first moment (one kawn) and subsequent moments (two kawns).

Key Terms
جوهر (substance)
حركة (motion)
سكون (rest)
كون (kawn)
عرض (accident)
حالة البقاء (state of remaining)
أول أحوال الوجود (first state of existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a substance stays in the same place at the first moment, that should be considered rest.
Correction: Rest requires two kawns (being in one place at two times). The first moment has only one kawn, so it cannot be rest.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many 'kawns' are required for a substance to be considered in motion or at rest in the state of remaining?
A) One kawn
B) Two kawns
C) Three kawns
D) None

Key Takeaway
The first moment of a substance's existence has only one 'kawn', making it neither motion nor rest, and this establishes the substance's temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your very first moment of existence was a single 'kawn' – placed, not moving, not at rest. You did not choose where or when to begin. If that origin was entirely given and you contributed nothing to it, then how can you claim ownership over your life as if you created yourself? What would change if you lived today with the constant awareness that you are radically contingent, dependent at every moment? Will you spend even five minutes after this lesson sitting with the reality that your existence is a gift, not a right?
?
After that first moment, every instant of your life has been either motion (changing) or rest (staying). You have never had a single moment that was not one of these two. This means you are constantly either moving toward something or standing still. What are you moving toward right now? What are you resting in? Are you comfortable resting in that which fades, or will you set yourself in motion toward the Eternal? Do not let this reflection pass without a concrete intention: name one specific, immediate change in your direction or stillness.

Resolving the Doubt: The True Scope of the Proof of Tawhid
The author resolves a doubt about the proof of tawhid by distinguishing between invalidating Allah's lordship and proving the impossibility of a second god.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof against multiple gods does not invalidate Allah's established lordship but rather shows the impossibility of a second god.

Core Idea
The proof of tawhid negates a second god, not the One God.

The author responds to a cunning doubt: if the proof of tawhid were applied, it would seem to invalidate both gods—or leave one powerless. This confusion arises from not distinguishing between an established lordship and a hypothetical second. In this passage, Sanusi clarifies the correct logical structure of the proof and why it poses no threat to Allah's divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and the initial response
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how the author starts by dismissing the doubt as deception.
The doubt is that the proof would invalidate both or one lordship, which is impossible.
2
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes between invalidating an established lordship (impossible) and proving the impossibility of a second god (possible).
This is the core of the resolution.
3
See the method of the proof
The proof first establishes one Creator via contingent things, then considers whether a second is possible, concluding it is impossible due to conflict leading to powerlessness.
The proof does not touch Allah's established lordship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول له: أما حل الشبهة أن ما ذكرتم من الدلالة يخرجهما أو أحدهما عن الربوبية، وذلك محال كما قلتم فى الواحد أنه لا يوصف بالقدرة على الكذب، فهو أن هذا تمويه فإن المستحيل هو إبطال ربوبية من ثبتت ربوبيته ولم تثبت ربوبية اثنين ليكون ما يوجب بطلان ذلك مستحيلا بل علم ثبوت الصانع للعالم بدلالة المحدثات، ثم أخطرنا بالبال أن صانعا آخر جائز الوجود أو ممتنع الوجود، فتأملنا فعلمنا أنه ممتنع الوجود لما مر أنه لو كان مع الله إله آخر لكانا جميعا عاجزين أو كان أحدهما عاجزا، والعاجز لا يصلح أن يكون إلها، فكان ذلك دليل أن لا يتصور ثبوت صانعين، فلم يكن هذا دليلا يوجب بطلان ربوبية من ثبتت ربوبيته بل ذلك دليل استحالة ثبوت صانعين بخلاف صانع واحد، لأنه تثبت ربوبيته بالدليل الموجب، وبطلان ربوبية من تثبت ربوبيته محال لكون ربوبيته واجب الوجود مستحيل العدم فمن لم يعرف الفرق بين إبطال ما هو مستحيل البطلان وبين امتناع ثبوت ما وجد دليل امتناعه فهو قليل الحظ من العلم بالحقائق والذي يدل على بطلان كلامه وبعد استشهاده أنه شبه هذا باستحالة ثبوت القدرة على ظلمه أو كذبه وهو غاية الجهالة، فإنا صورنا ثبوت قدرة كل واحد منهما على ما لا يستحيل ثبوت القدرة عليه، لولا منع صاحبه إياه، فإن أحدهما إذا أراد إثبات السواد في محل أو إثبات الحركة والحياة فيه فقد أراد إثبات ما هو مقدور في نفسه، إذ السواد والحركة والحياة داخلة تحت القدرة لولا تحصیل صاحبه أضدادها، فحيث لم يقدر على إثباتها فإنما لم يقدر لمنع صاحبه إياه عن تنفيذ مراده.
Then we say to him: As for the resolution of the doubt: that what you mentioned from the proof would remove both of them or one of them from divinity, and that is impossible, just as you said regarding the One that He is not described as having power over lying. This is a deception, for the impossible is the invalidation of the lordship of one whose lordship is established, while the lordship of two has not been established such that what necessitates its invalidation would be impossible. Rather, we know the existence of the Maker of the world through the evidence of contingent things. Then we considered that another maker is either possible of existence or impossible of existence. We reflected and knew that he is impossible of existence because, as has passed, if there were another god with Allah, they would both be powerless or one of them would be powerless, and the powerless is not fit to be a god. Thus that was evidence that the existence of two makers is inconceivable. So this is not a proof that necessitates the invalidation of the lordship of one whose lordship is established, but rather it is a proof of the impossibility of the existence of two makers, unlike one Maker, because His lordship is established by a positive proof, and the invalidation of the lordship of one whose lordship is established is impossible because His lordship is necessary of existence, impossible of non-existence. Whoever does not know the difference between invalidating what is impossible to be invalidated and the impossibility of the existence of something whose impossibility has been proven is one of little share in the knowledge of realities. What indicates the falsehood of his statement and the remoteness of his citation is that he analogized this to the impossibility of the existence of power over injustice or lying, and that is the utmost ignorance, for we have conceived the existence of power for each of them over what it is not impossible to have power over, were it not for the prevention of the other. For if one of them wills to posit blackness in a place or to posit motion and life in it, he wills to posit something that is possible in itself, since blackness, motion, and life are within [the scope of] power were it not for the other's bringing of their opposites. So where he is unable to posit them, he is only unable due to the other's prevention of him from executing his will.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is the author's response to a doubt (shubha) raised by the opponent. The doubt is that the Quranic proof (e.g., 'if there were gods besides Allah, they would corrupt') would invalidate the lordship of both or one of them, which is impossible—just as it is impossible for Allah to have power over lying. The author clarifies that this is a confusion: the impossible is to invalidate an established lordship (Allah's), but here the lordship of a second god was never established. The method is: first prove the existence of one Creator through contingent things, then consider whether a second is possible. Through reflection, we conclude it is impossible because if there were two, either both would be powerless or one would be powerless (due to conflict), and a powerless being cannot be a god. Thus the proof shows the impossibility of a second god, not the invalidity of the one true God. The author distinguishes between invalidating what cannot be invalidated (Allah's lordship) and proving the impossibility of something that was never established (a second god). He further refutes the analogy with power over injustice: in the case of two gods, each has power over what is possible in itself (like creating blackness), but their power is limited by the other's contrary action—prevention, not inherent impossibility. This differs from Allah's power over lying, which is inherently impossible.

Key Terms
شبهة
ربوبية
صانع
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of tawhid would invalidate Allah's lordship if it were applied.
Correction: The proof only shows the impossibility of a second god; it does not affect Allah's established lordship, which is necessary and cannot be invalidated.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction the author makes in resolving the doubt about the proof of two gods?
A) The proof invalidates the lordship of both gods equally.
B) The proof shows the impossibility of a second god, not the invalidation of the established lordship of Allah.
C) The proof is only valid for the Mu'tazila.
D) The proof was revealed only for the polytheists.

Key Takeaway
The proof against multiple gods does not invalidate Allah's established lordship; it proves the impossibility of a second god.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you explain the proof against multiple gods, do you ever worry that someone might think you are attacking the very concept of God? How would you respond to that confusion inside your own heart?
?
The author says that whoever does not distinguish between these two matters has 'little share in knowledge of realities'. Are you someone who, in your own understanding, has grasped this distinction firmly? If not, what will you do to ensure it?
?
Imagine you are in a discussion with someone who argues that the tamānu' argument proves the existence of God. How would you gently lead them to see the proper order of proofs—and does your own reliance on that argument show that you might be falling into the same confusion?

Refuting the Analogy: Power Over Lying vs. The Inability of Two Gods
The author refutes the analogy between the two-god proof and the claim that Allah cannot have power over lying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between the impossibility of two gods and Allah's inability to lie is invalid, and how prevention differs from inherent impossibility.

Core Idea
Two gods would prevent each other, not lack power—unlike lying which is inherently impossible.

The opponent tries to compare the argument against two gods to the statement that Allah is not described as having power over lying. The author shows this is a false analogy: in the case of two gods, each has power over what is possible in itself, but their power is limited by the other's contrary action. Prevention is not the same as inherent impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy and the author's refutation
Read the second passage carefully. The opponent analogized the two-god proof to Allah's inability to lie. The author calls this 'utmost ignorance'.
Focus on the concept of prevention (man') vs. impossibility (istihala).
2
Understand the concept of prevention
The author illustrates: each god has power over creating blackness, motion, etc., which are possible in themselves, but the other god can bring their opposites, preventing the first from actualizing his will.
The inability here is due to external prevention, not a lack of power.
3
Compare with the case of lying
Allah's inability to lie is because lying is inherently impossible for Him, not because something prevents Him. Thus the analogy fails.
The two cases are fundamentally different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول له: أما حل الشبهة أن ما ذكرتم من الدلالة يخرجهما أو أحدهما عن الربوبية، وذلك محال كما قلتم فى الواحد أنه لا يوصف بالقدرة على الكذب، فهو أن هذا تمويه فإن المستحيل هو إبطال ربوبية من ثبتت ربوبيته ولم تثبت ربوبية اثنين ليكون ما يوجب بطلان ذلك مستحيلا بل علم ثبوت الصانع للعالم بدلالة المحدثات، ثم أخطرنا بالبال أن صانعا آخر جائز الوجود أو ممتنع الوجود، فتأملنا فعلمنا أنه ممتنع الوجود لما مر أنه لو كان مع الله إله آخر لكانا جميعا عاجزين أو كان أحدهما عاجزا، والعاجز لا يصلح أن يكون إلها، فكان ذلك دليل أن لا يتصور ثبوت صانعين، فلم يكن هذا دليلا يوجب بطلان ربوبية من ثبتت ربوبيته بل ذلك دليل استحالة ثبوت صانعين بخلاف صانع واحد، لأنه تثبت ربوبيته بالدليل الموجب، وبطلان ربوبية من تثبت ربوبيته محال لكون ربوبيته واجب الوجود مستحيل العدم فمن لم يعرف الفرق بين إبطال ما هو مستحيل البطلان وبين امتناع ثبوت ما وجد دليل امتناعه فهو قليل الحظ من العلم بالحقائق والذي يدل على بطلان كلامه وبعد استشهاده أنه شبه هذا باستحالة ثبوت القدرة على ظلمه أو كذبه وهو غاية الجهالة، فإنا صورنا ثبوت قدرة كل واحد منهما على ما لا يستحيل ثبوت القدرة عليه، لولا منع صاحبه إياه، فإن أحدهما إذا أراد إثبات السواد في محل أو إثبات الحركة والحياة فيه فقد أراد إثبات ما هو مقدور في نفسه، إذ السواد والحركة والحياة داخلة تحت القدرة لولا تحصیل صاحبه أضدادها، فحيث لم يقدر على إثباتها فإنما لم يقدر لمنع صاحبه إياه عن تنفيذ مراده.
Then we say to him: As for the resolution of the doubt: that what you mentioned from the proof would remove both of them or one of them from divinity, and that is impossible, just as you said regarding the One that He is not described as having power over lying. This is a deception, for the impossible is the invalidation of the lordship of one whose lordship is established, while the lordship of two has not been established such that what necessitates its invalidation would be impossible. Rather, we know the existence of the Maker of the world through the evidence of contingent things. Then we considered that another maker is either possible of existence or impossible of existence. We reflected and knew that he is impossible of existence because, as has passed, if there were another god with Allah, they would both be powerless or one of them would be powerless, and the powerless is not fit to be a god. Thus that was evidence that the existence of two makers is inconceivable. So this is not a proof that necessitates the invalidation of the lordship of one whose lordship is established, but rather it is a proof of the impossibility of the existence of two makers, unlike one Maker, because His lordship is established by a positive proof, and the invalidation of the lordship of one whose lordship is established is impossible because His lordship is necessary of existence, impossible of non-existence. Whoever does not know the difference between invalidating what is impossible to be invalidated and the impossibility of the existence of something whose impossibility has been proven is one of little share in the knowledge of realities. What indicates the falsehood of his statement and the remoteness of his citation is that he analogized this to the impossibility of the existence of power over injustice or lying, and that is the utmost ignorance, for we have conceived the existence of power for each of them over what it is not impossible to have power over, were it not for the prevention of the other. For if one of them wills to posit blackness in a place or to posit motion and life in it, he wills to posit something that is possible in itself, since blackness, motion, and life are within [the scope of] power were it not for the other's bringing of their opposites. So where he is unable to posit them, he is only unable due to the other's prevention of him from executing his will.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is the author's response to a doubt (shubha) raised by the opponent. The doubt is that the Quranic proof (e.g., 'if there were gods besides Allah, they would corrupt') would invalidate the lordship of both or one of them, which is impossible—just as it is impossible for Allah to have power over lying. The author clarifies that this is a confusion: the impossible is to invalidate an established lordship (Allah's), but here the lordship of a second god was never established. The method is: first prove the existence of one Creator through contingent things, then consider whether a second is possible. Through reflection, we conclude it is impossible because if there were two, either both would be powerless or one would be powerless (due to conflict), and a powerless being cannot be a god. Thus the proof shows the impossibility of a second god, not the invalidity of the one true God. The author distinguishes between invalidating what cannot be invalidated (Allah's lordship) and proving the impossibility of something that was never established (a second god). He further refutes the analogy with power over injustice: in the case of two gods, each has power over what is possible in itself (like creating blackness), but their power is limited by the other's contrary action—prevention, not inherent impossibility. This differs from Allah's power over lying, which is inherently impossible.

Key Terms
قدرة
عاجز
تمويه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The inability of two gods to act is analogous to Allah's inability to lie.
Correction: The inability of two gods is due to mutual prevention of possible acts, whereas Allah's inability to lie is due to the inherent impossibility of lying for Him.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the analogy with Allah's inability to lie invalid?
A) Because two gods would each have power over impossible things.
B) Because the inability of two gods comes from mutual prevention, not inherent impossibility.
C) Because lying is possible for Allah.
D) Because the analogy only applies to the Mu'tazila.

Key Takeaway
The inability of two gods to act is due to mutual hindrance, not an inherent lack of power, unlike the impossibility of lying.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that Allah's inability to lie is a limitation similar to human weakness? How does this passage reframe that inability as a perfection? What trust does it require you to place in His words?
?
The opponent made an analogy that seemed sensible but was fundamentally flawed. Can you identify an analogy about Allah or His attributes that you have accepted without critical thought? What step can you take today to investigate if it aligns with sound theology?
?
If two gods would prevent each other, then your security rests solely in the oneness of Allah. In what area of your life are you acting as if there are competing powers (e.g., fear of people, reliance on wealth)? How does this lesson call you to realign your trust exclusively in the One who is completely able and unchallenged?

Accidents Prove Light and Darkness are Temporal
Argues that mixing and separation are accidents, proving light and darkness are temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the occurrence of accidents (mixing and separation) demonstrates that light and darkness are temporal, not eternal, refuting dualist claims.

Core Idea
Accidents like mixing and separation prove that light and darkness cannot be eternal.

This passage builds on the earlier proof for the existence of accidents. Imam Nasafi now applies that proof to refute the dualist view that light and darkness are eternal. He argues that mixing and separation are accidents that occur in light and darkness, and since nothing that precedes accidents can be eternal, light and darkness must be temporal.

Guided Path
1
Recall the proof for accidents
Remember that the author previously established the existence of accidents. This lesson applies that foundation to light and darkness.
The proof for accidents is the basis for the entire argument.
2
Identify the two accidents
Notice that mixing and separation are presented as two accidental meanings separate from the essences of light and darkness.
Tabayun (separation) and imtizaj (mixing) are the key terms.
3
Understand the temporal argument
Observe that mixing is observed to occur, and separation is known by its potential for non-existence. Anything that does not precede an accident is itself accidental (temporal).
This is a standard kalam principle: whatever is never free of accidents is itself an accident.
4
Extend the argument
The author then states that the same proofs for temporality of all bodies apply to light and darkness, and references earlier points about creation ex nihilo and evil.
This shows the comprehensive nature of the kalam cosmological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما أقمنا من الدلالة على ثبوت الأعراض فهو الدليل على كون التباين والامتزاج معنيين وراء ذاتيهما، وقد حدث الامتزاج في الحس، وعرف حدوث التباين بقبول العدم، فكانا معنيين حادثين، والنور والظلمة لا يخلوان عنهما ولا يسبقانهما، وقد مر أن ما لا يسبق الحادث فهو حادث، وكل دلیل عرف به حدوث سائر الأجسام فذلك الدليل موجود في حق هذين الجسمين، وقد سبق في الكلام ما يدل على جواز وجود الشيء لا من شيء، وعلى جواز إيجاد القبيح وكونه حكمة عند تعلق العاقبة الحميدة به على ما مر في إبطال كلام المجوس.
And what we have established of the evidence for the existence of accidents is the proof that separation and mixing are two meanings beyond their essences. Mixing has occurred in the sensible world, and the occurrence of separation is known by its susceptibility to non-existence. Thus they are two accidental meanings. Light and darkness are not free of them nor do they precede them. It has been established that whatever does not precede an accident is itself an accident. And every proof by which the temporality of other bodies is known, that proof applies to these two bodies. It has preceded in the discussion what indicates the permissibility of a thing coming into existence from nothing, and the permissibility of creating evil and it being wisdom when a praiseworthy consequence is attached to it, as has preceded in the refutation of the speech of the Magians.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage provides a refutation of the dualists' claim that light and darkness are eternal. The author argues that the evidence for the existence of accidents proves that separation (tabayun) and mixing (imtizaj) are two accidental meanings (ma'nayan hadithan) that are distinct from the essences (dhawat) of light and darkness. Since mixing is observed to occur, and separation is known to be subject to annihilation, both are temporally originated (hadith). Light and darkness cannot be free of these accidents nor do they precede them. Following a standard kalām principle, anything that does not precede an accident is itself an accident. Therefore, the proofs for the temporality of all bodies apply to these two bodies as well. The author also references earlier discussions on creation ex nihilo and the permissibility of evil being a wisdom when its ultimate consequence is praiseworthy, as was argued against the Magians.

Key Terms

التباين
الامتزاج
الحادث
القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument is based solely on empirical observation of mixing and separation.
Correction: While observation is used, the core proof is a rational principle: anything that does not precede an accident is itself temporal. The empirical observation only confirms that mixing and separation are accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the author's refutation, why does the occurrence of mixing prove that light and darkness are not eternal?
A) Because mixing causes change in eternal beings.
B) Because mixing is an accident, and what is subject to accidents must be temporal.
C) Because light and darkness are bodies.
D) Because the senses perceive mixing.

Key Takeaway
Light and darkness cannot be eternal because they are always accompanied by temporal accidents (mixing and separation), and whatever does not precede an accident is itself temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you unconsciously treat something in your life as if it were eternal—your health, a relationship, or your station in life? This lesson proves that even light and darkness, the most fundamental realities you perceive, are temporal and created. If you truly believed that everything except Allah is passing away, what would you prioritize today?
?
The passage argues that mixing and separation are accidents that prove temporality. Think of the 'mixing' of good and evil in your own story. Can you accept that the evil you have experienced is not eternal but is a temporary accident that Allah created for a praiseworthy outcome? How would embracing this change the way you carry that pain?
?
If both light and darkness are created and dependent on Allah, then neither is independent. Is there any area of your life where you act as if a created thing—your intellect, your money, your reputation—is self-sufficient and beyond the reach of temporality? What concrete step can you take today to affirm its utter reliance on the Creator?

Contradiction in Dualist Claim: Mixing After Separation Implies Change
Points out the contradiction that mixing after separation involves change, impossible for the eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dualists' claim that mixing occurs after separation is self-contradictory, because mixing implies change and change is impossible for the eternal.

Core Idea
Mixing after separation implies change, which is impossible for the eternal.

This passage presents a powerful logical argument against the dualist worldview, which asserts that eternal principles like light and darkness can mix. The author demonstrates that such mixing would entail change, and change is a sign of temporality—something impossible for an eternal being. This lesson unpacks that argument and its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the argument's flow. Don't analyze yet—just let the words sink in.
The passage is short but dense. Pay attention to the key terms.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice that the author states there are several contradictions ('min wujuh'), but only one is given. The structure is: claim → implication (mixing after separation = change) → theological rule (change impossible for eternal) → conclusion (contradiction).
The rule 'change is a sign of temporality' is crucial.
3
Understand why change is impossible for the eternal
Reflect on the concept of 'eternal' (qadim). If something is eternal, it has no beginning and is not subject to change. Change implies a before and after, which is a sign of temporality (hadath).
This is based on the principle that anything that changes is contingent.
4
Connect to the larger theological context
Consider how this argument defends the transcendence of Allah (the true Eternal) by rejecting any possibility of change in Him. The dualists' failure to respect this leads to contradiction.
This passage is part of a broader refutation of dualist metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم ناقضوا في القول بالامتزاج من وجوه أحدها ـ أن الامتزاج بعد البينونة تغير وهو مستحيل على القديم لما هو من أمارات الحدث وثبوت أمارات الحدث في القديم محال وإثباته تناقض لما فيه من القول بالقدم والحدوث معا.
Then they contradict (themselves) concerning the claim of mixing in several respects. One of them: that mixing after separation is a change, and change is impossible for the eternal because it is a sign of temporality. The establishment of signs of temporality in the eternal is impossible, and affirming it is a contradiction because it involves affirming both pre-eternity and temporality together.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage highlights a specific contradiction in the dualists' claim about mixing. The author states that the dualists contradict themselves in several ways. One of these contradictions is that mixing after separation constitutes a change (taghayyur). Change is a sign of temporality (hadath) and is impossible for an eternal being (qadim). Affirming change in an eternal being would require affirming both pre-eternity and temporality together, which is self-contradictory.

Key Terms
الامتزاج
البينونة
التغير

أمارات الحدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dualists' contradiction is simply a logical fallacy that could be resolved by redefining terms.
Correction: The contradiction is rooted in the very nature of eternity and temporality. Change is not just a logical inconsistency but a theological impossibility for the eternal, because change implies contingency and temporal dependence.

Checkpoint

A) They claim mixing occurs but also claim separation is permanent.
B) They claim mixing after separation, which implies change in the eternal.
C) They claim both light and darkness are eternal but also created.
D) They claim mixing is a natural process.

Key Takeaway
The dualists' claim that mixing occurs after separation implies change, which contradicts the eternal nature they assert for the principles, making their position self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The dualists claimed mixing after separation—an impossibility for the eternal. Where in your life do you try to mix what is incompatible (e.g., pursuing both worldly success and sincere devotion without checking which truly dominates)? Will you examine that contradiction today?

The Ugliness of Killing: Dialectical Refutation
Dialectical refutation of the claim that killing is inherently ugly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that killing is inherently ugly by distinguishing between sound intellect and weak nature.

Core Idea
Killing's ugliness is not self-evident; it depends on nature and self-concern.

In this passage, the author engages in a dialectical argument against those who claim killing is inherently ugly. By asking a key question, the author shows that the supposed ugliness is not objective but subjective. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the dialectical structure come through.
2
Identify the dialectical structure
Notice that the author presents a doubt, then asks a question with two possible answers, and responds to each.
3
Analyze the first answer and response
The first answer is 'killing is ugly in itself.' The author's response is 'This is denied' – meaning it is not self-evident.
4
Analyze the second answer and response
The second answer is 'sound intellects are repulsed.' The author counters that it is only foolish natures and weak hearts that are repulsed due to self-concern.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the author concludes that ugliness is not inherent but relative to one's nature and self-interest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشبهتهم التي يتسلقون بها على أرباب الديانات من إباحة الذبح والتقرب بالقرابين تندفع بهذا ثم نقول لهم : أتزعمون أن القتل قبيح لعينه أم لمعنى فيه؟ فإن قالوا: هو قبيح لعينه، قيل : هذا ممنوع، وإن قالوا: تنفر عنه العقول السليمة، قلنا ليس كذلك بل تنفر عنه الطبائع السخيفة والقلوب الضعيفة لما تقدر كل حلولها في نفسه، فتنفر عنه طبيعته، ولهذا يسهل ذلك على طبيعة من اتخذ ذلك.
And their doubt by which they climb against the people of religions regarding the permissibility of slaughtering and drawing near through sacrifices is repelled by this. Then we say to them: Do you claim that killing is ugly in itself (qabih li-'aynihi) or due to a meaning in it? If they say: It is ugly in itself, it is said: This is denied (mamnu'). If they say: Sound intellects are repulsed by it, we say: It is not so; rather, foolish natures and weak hearts are repulsed by it, because each one estimates its occurrence in himself, so his nature is repulsed by it. And for this reason it becomes easy for the nature of one who has adopted that [i.e., killing].
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument against the dualist claim that killing is inherently ugly (qabih li'aynihi). The author asks whether killing is ugly in itself or due to some meaning. If they say it is ugly in itself, that is denied (as not self-evident). If they say sound intellects repudiate it, the author counters that it is not sound intellect but rather foolish natures and weak hearts that are repulsed, due to self-concern. Thus the ugliness is not objective but subjective, as evidenced by those who find killing easy.

Key Terms
قبيح لعينه
معنى
عقول سليمة
طبائع سخيفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the ugliness of killing is obvious to all intellects, and that anyone who permits killing is irrational.
Correction: The author argues that it is not sound intellect but foolish natures and weak hearts that are repulsed by killing, due to self-concern. Those with different natures (e.g., warriors) may not find it ugly.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the claim that sound intellects are repulsed by killing?
A) It is true.
B) It is only foolish natures that are repulsed.
C) Killing is always ugly.
D) Only prophets permit killing.

Key Takeaway
Killing's ugliness is not inherent but depends on the observer's nature and self-interest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel repulsed by an action, do you immediately assume it is your sound intellect reacting? Reflect on a recent judgment you made about something being wrong. Was it truly objective, or were you imagining yourself in that situation? How can you train yourself to distinguish self-concern from genuine moral discernment? Commit to examining one such reaction today.
?
The passage says that those who have adopted killing find it easy. Consider a practice in your life that you once found difficult but now find easy. Could it be that your conscience has been dulled by repetition, not refined by truth? Are there actions you have normalized that your conscience once questioned? Identify one and take a concrete step to re-evaluate it.
?
If the ugliness of killing is not inherent but depends on nature, then what is the measure of a 'sound' nature? How does your own nature align with the standard of sound intellect as defined by revelation? What specific change in your daily habits would bring your nature closer to that standard? Write down one action you will take this week.

Why Killing Is Not Inherently Evil: Reason, Nature, and Habituation
Argues that killing is not qabīḥ li-ʻaynh but depends on reason and habituation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that killing is not inherently evil (qabīḥ li-ʻaynih) but rather its moral judgment depends on reason and habituation.

Core Idea
Killing is ugly by habit, not by essence; reason judges the essence, nature bends with habit.

This passage confronts the objection that killing (e.g., animal sacrifice) must be evil because people recoil from it. The author uses a rational dilemma to show that if killing were inherently evil, it would never become acceptable; yet we see that habit makes it easy. The distinction between what reason deems inherently evil and what custom rejects is key.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the structure: the dilemma, the denial, the rebuttal about nature vs. intellect, and the role of habituation.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Notice the question: is killing ugly in itself or due to a meaning? The author denies the first and reinterprets the second.
The opponent may claim either: it is ugly in itself, or that sound intellects recoil.
3
Understand the nature vs. intellect distinction
The author contrasts 'nature' (which can change) with 'intellect' (which sees realities). Killing is not evil to intellect, only to base nature.
Nature recoils due to selfish fear, not rational judgment.
4
Reflect on habituation
See how the author uses habituation to show that initial repulsion can be overcome, proving that the ugliness is not essential.
Habituation changes nature, but it cannot change what intellect judges inherently evil.
5
Connect to the broader argument
This supports the permissibility of animal sacrifice against dualist objections, establishing that killing is not inherently evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشبهتهم التي يتسلقون بها على أرباب الديانات من إباحة الذبح والتقرب بالقرابين تندفع بهذا ثم نقول لهم أتزعمون أن القتل قبيح لعينه أم لمعنى فيه؟ فإن قالوا هو قبيح لعينه قيل هذا ممنوع، وإن قالوا تنفر عنه العقول السليمة قلنا ليس كذلك بل تنفر عنه الطبائع السخيفة والقلوب الضعيفة لما تقدر كل حلولها في نفسه، فتنفر عنه طبيعته، ولهذا يسهل ذلك على طبيعة من اتخذ ذلك عادة، والطبيعة هي التي يسهل عليها بالاعتياد حتى يصير متألفا بما كان تنفر عنه قبل الاعتياد فأما ما استقبحه العقل لذاته فلا يحسن ذلك فيه البتة، إذ هو يرى الأشياء بحقائقها لا الطبيعة
And their doubt by which they climb upon the followers of religions regarding the permissibility of slaughter and drawing close with offerings is repelled by this. Then we say to them: Do you claim that killing is ugly in itself or due to a meaning in it? If they say it is ugly in itself, it is said: this is denied. And if they say: sound intellects recoil from it, we say: not so; rather, vile natures and weak hearts recoil from it because each one imagines that it might happen to himself, so his nature recoils from it. And for this reason it becomes easy for the nature of one who has taken it as a habit. And nature is that which becomes easy through habituation, until it becomes accustomed to what it used to recoil from before habituation. As for what the intellect deems ugly in itself, that never becomes good in it at all, because it sees things according to their realities, not nature.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument about the ethical status of killing. The author first dismisses the dualist doubt about animal sacrifice, then poses a dilemma: is killing ugly in itself (li-ʻaynh) or due to some meaning (li-maʻnan)? If the opponent says li-ʻaynih, the author denies it. If the opponent says sound intellects recoil from it, the author argues that it is not sound intellect but base natures and weak hearts that recoil, due to selfish fear. He then introduces the role of habituation: nature can change through habit, so what initially repulses becomes familiar. Finally, he distinguishes between what the intellect judges ugly inherently—which never becomes good—and what is only customarily rejected. This establishes that killing is not inherently evil.

Key Terms
قبيح لعينه
قبيح لمعنى
العقول السليمة
الطبيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is arguing that all killing is permissible.
Correction: The author only argues that killing is not inherently evil; its moral status depends on reason and context. He does not claim blanket permissibility.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why do people initially recoil from killing?
A) Because sound intellect recognizes it as inherently evil.
B) Because of base natures and weak hearts that fear for themselves.
C) Because all religions prohibit it.
D) Because it harms society.

Key Takeaway
Killing is not inherently evil; its moral status depends on rational judgment and habituation, not on innate repulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you have always considered evil. Is your revulsion based on rational conviction that it is inherently wrong, or is it rooted in your personal fears and social conditioning? Can you honestly separate the two?
?
The author says that nature recoils from killing because it imagines itself as the victim. When you feel disgust or horror at a violent act, do you find a trace of selfish fear in your reaction? How much of your moral outrage is about 'what if it happens to me'?
?
If rational judgment is stable but nature changes by habit, where do you stand today? Have you allowed your habits to override what your intellect tells you is right? What is one habit you need to change to align your nature with your reasoned convictions?

The Serpent and the Thief: Context Makes the Act
Argument that killing can be good with context and authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the example of killing a dangerous serpent or a thief demonstrates that killing is not inherently evil but becomes good based on context and authority.

Core Idea
Killing is not evil in itself; its morality depends on context and authority.

This lesson presents a classic Maturidi argument for the contextual nature of moral judgments. By examining the scenario of killing a serpent or a thief to save lives, the author shows that the same act can be good or evil depending on the circumstances. This prepares us for a deeper understanding of how divine command and rational evaluation interact.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about every detail. Then read the full translation. Notice the dialectical style: the author poses a scenario, asks a question, and forces the opponent to concede a point.
The text ends abruptly with 'بطلت' but the main argument is complete.
2
Analyze the hypothetical scenario
Focus on the example of the serpent or lion entering a town and killing people. The author asks: would killing it be good? The opponent must say yes, which proves that killing is not always evil.
This is the core 'proof by example'.
3
Understand the deeper reasoning
After the example, the author explains that if killing were ugly, it would be due to lack of authority, lack of ownership, or divine prohibition. Since God owns everything and can permit or forbid, killing becomes good when a good context is added.
The key terms are 'ولاية' (authority) and 'قرينة' (context).
4
Reflect on the challenge to opponents
The author challenges opponents who say they would know if a context existed. He asks: is everything you don't know nonexistent? Their answer either shows obstinacy or invalidates their objection.
This is a dialectical finishing move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : أرأيتم لو أن ثعباناً عظيماً دخلت بلدة فيها خلق كثير أو أسدا ضاريا اقتحم مدينة من المدائن وجعل يقتل الناس يمينا وشمالا، وكان بحال لو لم يقتل لأتى على جميع أهل تلك البلدة، أكان قتل الثعبان أو الأسد قبل إفنائه الخلق مستحسنا أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: لا، فقد ظهرت مكابرتهم، وإن قالوا: نعم عرف أن القتل ليس بقبيح لعينه أو حسن مع وجود عينه، وكذا هذه المطالبة في قتل اللصوص وقطاع الطريق الذين قتلهم يسبب إصلاح العالم وسكون لدهماء الناس وأمن عن إثارة الفتنة ثم إذا ظهر أن عينه ليس بقبيح فلو قبح إنما قبح لما أن القاتل لا ولاية على المقتول، أو لا ملك له فيه أو منعه من له الخلق والأمر عن قتله. والله تعالى له الملك في الأشياء بأجمعها ، يتصرف فيها كيف يشاء فيحظر إتلافها تارة ويبيح ذلك أخرى على قدر ما يعلم فيه من الحكمة لا دافع لقضائه ولا معارض لمشيئته. ثم بعدما ثبت أن القتل ليس بقبيح لذاته بل لو قبح لقبح بانضمام قرينة إليه، وما هذا سبيله، يحسن بانضمام قرينة مستحسنة إليه على ما قررنا في قتل الثعبان والأسد ثم يقال لهم ما أنكرتم أن قتل هذه الحيوانات لا يكون مستقبحا إلى بعض الأحوال، فتجوز إباحته ويصير مستحسنا لانضمام قرينة إليه فيجوز إيجابه، فإن قالوا لو انضمت إليه القرينة لعرفناها، قلنا لهم: أفكل ما لا تعرفون ليس بموجود؟ فإن قالوا: نعم ظهرت مكابرتهم ، وإن قالوا: لا، بطلت
Then we say: Do you not see that if a great serpent entered a town with many people, or a fierce lion broke into a city and started killing people left and right, and it was such that if it were not killed it would destroy all the inhabitants of that town, would killing the serpent or the lion before it annihilates the people be considered good or not? If they say no, then their obstinacy is shown. If they say yes, it is known that killing is not ugly in itself, or that it is good despite its essence. Similarly, this applies to killing thieves and highway robbers, whose killing causes the reformation of the world, the tranquility of the common people, and security from inciting fitna. Then when it is clear that its essence is not ugly, if it were ugly, it would only be ugly because the killer has no authority over the killed, or has no ownership over him, or because the One Who created and commanded forbids his killing. And God Almighty owns all things; He disposes them as He wills, forbidding their destruction sometimes and permitting it at other times according to what He knows of wisdom; there is no repeller of His decree and no opponent to His will. Then after it is established that killing is not ugly in itself, but if it were ugly it would be ugly due to a context, and what is of this nature becomes good when a good context is added to it, as we established regarding the killing of the serpent and the lion. Then it is said to them: What prevents you from acknowledging that killing these animals is not considered ugly in some situations, so it is permissible and becomes good due to the addition of a good context, and may become obligatory. If they say: If a context were added, we would know it. We say to them: Is everything you do not know nonexistent? If they say yes, their obstinacy is shown. If they say no, it is invalid.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage provides a concrete argument for the contextual nature of killing. The author poses a scenario: if a serpent or lion is killing many people and must be killed to save them, is that killing good? The opponent must admit it is good, proving that killing is not inherently evil. The same logic applies to executing thieves and bandits for societal benefit. Then the author analyzes why killing might be considered ugly: lack of authority or ownership, or divine prohibition. But God, who owns all things, can permit or forbid as He wills. Therefore, killing becomes good when a good context (qarīnah) is added. The author then challenges opponents: if they claim they would know such a context, he asks whether everything they do not know is nonexistent—if they say yes, they are obstinate; if they say no, their objection fails.

Key Terms
قبيح لعينه
قرينة
ولاية
الملك
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is justifying all forms of killing, including unjust murder.
Correction: The author is not justifying all killings; he is showing that the moral evaluation depends on authority and context, not on the act itself. Only when there is proper authority (like the state or self-defense) and a good context does killing become permissible or obligatory.

Checkpoint
What does the example of killing a serpent that threatens many people demonstrate?
A) Killing is always permissible.
B) Killing is not inherently evil, but can be good depending on context.
C) Serpents have no souls so killing them is trivial.
D) Only God can judge the morality of killing.

Key Takeaway
Killing is morally neutral in itself; its goodness or evil depends on context and the permission of the One with authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged an action as simply 'wrong' without considering whether God might permit it for a greater purpose? When? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
If God truly owns everything, including your life, what right do you have to claim absolute control over your own choices? Where do you need to submit to His authority today?
?
This lesson shows that even killing can be good with the right context. What firmly held belief or action in your life might you be holding as 'always evil' without considering God's wisdom? Will you ask Him to show you the truth?

Killing is Not Inherently Evil: The Snake and Lion Example
Nasafi argues that killing is not inherently evil, using the example of a dangerous snake or lion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi tradition holds that actions are not inherently good or evil, and how the example of a dangerous snake illustrates this principle.

Core Idea
Killing is not evil in itself; its moral value depends on context and divine command.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi tackles a fundamental question of Islamic theology: are actions intrinsically good or evil? He uses a vivid example of a deadly snake or lion threatening a community to argue that our moral judgments are shaped by circumstances, not by the action's essence. This sets the stage for understanding the Maturidi position on divine command and human reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally before analyzing it.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the definition of nature
Nasafi begins by defining 'nature' as habituation, contrasting it with the intellect which judges things as they truly are. Note that the intellect's judgment on inherent repugnance never changes.
This sets up the distinction between mutable and immutable moral judgments.
3
Analyze the snake/lion dilemma
Nasafi presents a hypothetical: if a snake or lion would kill everyone unless stopped, is killing it good or not? The opponent must admit it is good, proving killing is not inherently evil.
This is the core argument: circumstance transforms moral evaluation.
4
Trace the extension and qualification
Nasafi extends the reasoning to thieves and highwaymen, then clarifies that if killing were evil, it would be due to lack of authority or ownership. Since God has absolute ownership, He can permit or forbid as He wills.
The objection about not knowing the context is answered: ignorance does not entail non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطبيعة هي التي يسهل عليها بالاعتياد حتى يصير متألفا بما كان تنفر عنه قبل الاعتياد. فأما ما استقبحه العقل لذاته فلا يحسن ذلك فيه البتة، إذ هو يرى الأشياء بحقائقها لا الطبيعة. ثم نقول: أرأيتم لو أن ثعبانا عظيما دخلت بلدة فيها خلق كثير أو أسدا ضاريا اقتحم مدينة من المدائن وجعل يقتل الناس يمينا وشمالا، وكان بحال لو لم يقتل لأتى على جميع أهل تلك البلدة، أكان قتل الثعبان أو الأسد قبل إفنائه الخلق مستحسنا أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: لا، فقد ظهرت مكابرتهم، وإن قالوا: نعم عرف أن القتل ليس بقبيح لعينه أو حسن مع وجود عينه. وكذا هذه المطالبة في قتل اللصوص وقطاع الطريق الذين قتلهم يسبب إصلاح العالم وسكون لدهماء الناس وأمن عن إثارة الفتنة. ثم إذا ظهر أن عينه ليس بقبيح فلو قبح إنما قبح لما أن القاتل لا ولاية على المقتول، أو لا ملك له فيه أو منعه من له الخلق والأمر عن قتله. والله تعالى له الملك في الأشياء بأجمعها، يتصرف فيها كيف يشاء فيحظر إتلافها تارة ويبيح ذلك أخرى على قدر ما يعلم فيه من الحكمة لا دافع لقضائه ولا معارض لمشيئته. ثم بعدما ثبت أن القتل ليس بقبيح لذاته بل لو قبح لقبح بانضمام قرينة إليه، وما هذا سبيله، يحسن بانضمام قرينة مستحسنة إليه على ما قررنا في قتل الثعبان والأسد. ثم يقال لهم ما أنكرتم أن قتل هذه الحيوانات لا يكون مستقبحا إلى بعض الأحوال، فتجوز إباحته ويصير مستحسنا لانضمام قرينة إليه فيجوز إيجابه. فإن قالوا لو انضمت إليه القرينة لعرفناها، قلنا لهم: أفكل ما لا تعرفون ليس بموجود؟ فإن قالوا: نعم ظهرت مكابرتهم، وإن قالوا: لا، بطلت شبهتهم، وبالله التوفيق والعصمة.
And nature is that which becomes easy by habituation until it becomes familiar with what it recoiled from before habituation. As for what the intellect deems repugnant in itself, that never becomes good in it, for it sees things as they truly are, not nature. Then we say: Do you see that if a great serpent entered a town containing many people, or a harmful lion stormed a city and began killing people right and left, and it was such that if he did not kill, he would destroy all the people of that city—would killing the serpent or lion before it annihilated the people be considered good or not? If they say: No, then their obstinacy is evident. And if they say: Yes, it is known that killing is not evil in itself, nor is it good with its essence. And likewise this demand applies to killing thieves and highwaymen, whose killing causes the reform of the world, the tranquility of the common people, and security from stirring up sedition. Then, when it is established that its essence is not evil, if it were evil, it would only be evil because the killer has no authority over the killed, or no ownership over him, or because He who created and commands has forbidden his killing. And Allah Almighty has ownership over all things; He disposes of them as He wills, sometimes prohibiting their destruction and other times permitting it, according to what He knows of wisdom therein—none can repel His decree nor oppose His will. Then, after it is established that killing is not evil in itself, but if it were evil it would be evil due to an accompanying contextual factor, and what is this way, it becomes good by the conjunction of a good-making contextual factor, as we established regarding killing the serpent and the lion. Then it is said to them: What prevents you from [admitting] that killing these animals is not deemed evil in some states, so its permission is possible, and it becomes good due to the conjunction of a context, and so its obligation is possible? If they say: If a context were attached to it, we would know it, we say to them: Is everything you do not know non-existent? If they say: Yes, their obstinacy appears. And if they say: No, their argument is invalidated. And with Allah is success and protection.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a complex argumentation that begins with a definition and then proceeds to a proof. The author first defines 'nature' (al-tabi'a) as that which becomes easy by habituation, contrasting it with the intellect ('aql) which judges things as they truly are and never changes its judgment on what is inherently repugnant. Then he poses a dilemma: if a dangerous snake or lion threatens to kill all inhabitants of a town, is killing it before it destroys them good or not? If the opponent says it is not good, that is obstinate denial; if they say it is good, then they admit killing is not inherently evil (qabih li-dhatihi) but becomes good due to the circumstance. He extends this to thieves and highwaymen. He then clarifies that if killing were evil, it would be due to external factors like lack of authority. Since God has absolute ownership and authority, He can permit or forbid as He wills. The argument then answers a potential objection: if the opponent claims they would know the good-making context if it existed, the author retorts that not knowing does not mean non-existence, and if they persist, their obstinacy is exposed.

Key Terms
الطبيعة
العقل
قبيح لذاته
القرينة
الملك
الولاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument only applies to self-defense against deadly animals.
Correction: The argument is used to establish a general principle: moral values are not inherent in actions but derive from God's command and contextual factors, as shown with thieves and highwaymen.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the initial definition of 'nature' (al-tabi'a)?
A) That which is inherently good or evil.
B) That which becomes easy by habituation.
C) That which the intellect judges as repugnant.
D) That which is unchanging.

Key Takeaway
The moral value of an action is not intrinsic but depends on contextual factors and divine authorization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever condemned someone's action as evil without knowing the full context or Allah's command? Reflect on a specific instance and consider how this lesson challenges your judgment.
?
The argument uses the example of killing a dangerous animal to show that context changes moral value. Think of a situation in your life where you assumed something was always wrong but later realized it could be right under divine permission. How did that change your heart?
?
If moral values are not inherent but depend on Allah's will, are you willing to surrender your personal sense of right and wrong to whatever Allah commands, even if it initially feels repugnant to you? What would that look like in your daily decisions?
